  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_85e06448-b293-4e52-aab6-dcb3d412a694'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_1055101828" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1055101828" />
    <a title="Mark" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3511708-mark"><img alt="Mark" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1269889891p2/3511708.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1055101828?book_show_action=true">Sep 14, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mark" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mark" href="/user/show/3511708-mark">Mark</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3511708-mark?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1055101828" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8116362877928756879">Well, that was... something. Tad Williams, it seems, was ahead of his time in having the final volume of something have to be split across two volumes, as I actually read To Green Angel Tower across two paperbacks, each of which had 800 pages. It was a very long, meandering, interesting journey, at times bogged down by tedium, but at times full of tension, building up to a dramatic finale.<br/><br/>In writing about the first book of this series I mentioned that this is kind of like a brother to The Wheel </span>
  <span id="freeText8116362877928756879" style="display:none">Well, that was... something. Tad Williams, it seems, was ahead of his time in having the final volume of something have to be split across two volumes, as I actually read To Green Angel Tower across two paperbacks, each of which had 800 pages. It was a very long, meandering, interesting journey, at times bogged down by tedium, but at times full of tension, building up to a dramatic finale.<br /><br />In writing about the first book of this series I mentioned that this is kind of like a brother to The Wheel of Time and early Guy Gavriel Kay. Like, it's really interesting to see how it fits in the lineage of fantasy - you can see both the parts where it was obviously inspired by the likes of Tolkien and the parts where it has provided some inspiration to some of those who have come since in turn. For me, finding this trilogy years later, it's like looking into an old family photograph and seeing a picture of an uncle you'd never seen before. Maybe you only knew his name because it was mentioned in passing a couple of times. <br /><br />Then it's kind of stunning to see how he looks like the rest of the family but he's also his own person, and you kinda wish you knew what his story was. In real life if this happened you probably wouldn't know about this uncle because he's in jail or something, so obviously this analogy isn't perfect. Imagine if he walked through that door one day, though, and wanted to tell you all about his life story in great detail. Some things would bore you, but mostly you'd be really curious, and he's a good storyteller, your uncle, and since you've thought about him your whole life you'd want to hear about it all anyway. That's Memory, Sorrow, and Thorn.<br /><br />Epic fantasy, swords and sorcery and all that at some times, kind of a gothic horror at others, prose that gives off a legitimate sense of dread. It's a fun read, though it's dense; even a long book doesn't usually take me almost a month to plow through. That's not because it was boring, though, but because I wanted to savor it, and I did. It isn't something to rush through. There are many strands to the plot, all over the world, and unsurprisingly, they all come together in ways that I didn't expect, although of course I'm not exactly notorious as a plot guesser anyway.<br /><br />Cheesy at times, yes, wrapped up in fantasy cliches that were perhaps a bit more fresh in the late 1980s but have been tread many more times in the years since, sure. But then again, humans have told a lot of stories by now and it's hard to make something that's completely unlike anything that's been done before. What you can do is build a world that is all its own, with a rich history that is not just another Europe analogue, fill it up with characters, and make stuff happen. I liked the journey up the fantasy family tree.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8116362877928756879" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<span id="freeTextContainer8682892761102332622">A satisfying ending to an engaging trilogy. I can see why this final tome was originally published in two parts—it was a definite door-stop! I sprained my wrist two years ago, and I found that old injury aching at the end of lengthy reading sessions!<br/><br/>However, the size of the volume was necessary in order to tie up the many, many loose ends from the first two books. I especially appreciated the return of “Rachel the Dragon” as an honoured elder lady, even as I grieved the loss of other characters.</span>
  <span id="freeText8682892761102332622" style="display:none">A satisfying ending to an engaging trilogy. I can see why this final tome was originally published in two parts—it was a definite door-stop! I sprained my wrist two years ago, and I found that old injury aching at the end of lengthy reading sessions!<br /><br />However, the size of the volume was necessary in order to tie up the many, many loose ends from the first two books. I especially appreciated the return of “Rachel the Dragon” as an honoured elder lady, even as I grieved the loss of other characters. I also have to say that I appreciated the focus on Miriamele, despite the fact that she often came across as spoiled and irrational. I was able to endure that portrayal because Simon was often angry and petulant for no particular reason that I could discern either. Equal opportunity bad behaviour!<br /><br />I appreciated that Osten Ard was not just a clone of Middle Earth. Williams gave the world his own structure and rules, and created unique creatures and challenges for his characters. I really liked the ending--it worked for me. I always feel the tug of emotion as the war ends and the circle of friends must split up to return to their own lives—happy to get back to normal, sad to be parted.<br /><br /><b>Book number 267 in my Science Fiction &amp; Fantasy reading project.</b><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8682892761102332622" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer8787651590433798503">Phew! Three volumes and exactly one month later I finally finished this mammoth series. It’s a long, long journey but totally worth it.<br/><br/>Although it’s kind of LOTR copycat in terms of structure, it has its own original world, diverse characters and an intricate, multilayered plot. Peter Jackson would make a hell of a movie out of it.<br/><br/>This finale volume puts together all the pieces of the puzzle. Every loose thread is weaved in the big canvas.   <span id="freeText8787651590433798503" style="display:none">Phew! Three volumes and exactly one month later I finally finished this mammoth series. It’s a long, long journey but totally worth it.<br /><br />Although it’s kind of LOTR copycat in terms of structure, it has its own original world, diverse characters and an intricate, multilayered plot. Peter Jackson would make a hell of a movie out of it.<br /><br />This finale volume puts together all the pieces of the puzzle. Every loose thread is weaved in the big canvas. Being a classic fantasy <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[it has a happy ending <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> but not without sacrifices. Your heart will be melted, torn apart and put back together.<br /><br />As I said in the previous volume, the series is indeed very long, but I don’t think anything could have been removed because there is no infodump, nor details without importance. There is not a dull moment in the whole series and every little hint finds its closure in the end.<br /><br />Some people ask me why I read fantasy. It’s because it offers a bit of escapism, indeed, but that’s not the main reason. It’s because I love the worlds created, the valours – courage, friendship, loyalty -, love for nature and its ‘children’ and because good always prevails. And for I have a great time reading it - it’s so damn fun and exciting!<br /></span>

<div id="review_2704171939" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2704171939" />
    <a title="Matthew Quann" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22699228-matthew-quann"><img alt="Matthew Quann" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528393487p2/22699228.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2704171939?book_show_action=true">Mar 31, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matthew Quann" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matthew Quann" href="/user/show/22699228-matthew-quann">Matthew Quann</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 0756402983" href="/book/show/33898847-to-green-angel-tower">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22699228-matthew-quann?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22699228-matthew-quann?shelf=big-books-500-pages-plus">big-books-500-pages-plus</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2704171939" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7549404773350133711">Wrapping up more than 2000 pages of storytelling, <i>To Green Angel Tower</i> is a wholly satisfying conclusion to a super solid and influential fantasy series. Though I found the first book, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1867814715?book_show_action=false&from_review_page=1' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <i>The Dragonbone Chair</i>
</a>, to be a bit plodding with innumerable ancillary characters, by the time I started <i>To Green Angel Tower</i> I might as well have been preparing a thesis in Osten Ard's lore. It is at first a challenge to acclimate to the slow seduction and melancholy tone of Williams' world, but it is a challe</span>
  <span id="freeText7549404773350133711" style="display:none">Wrapping up more than 2000 pages of storytelling, <i>To Green Angel Tower</i> is a wholly satisfying conclusion to a super solid and influential fantasy series. Though I found the first book, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1867814715?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>The Dragonbone Chair</i>
</a>, to be a bit plodding with innumerable ancillary characters, by the time I started <i>To Green Angel Tower</i> I might as well have been preparing a thesis in Osten Ard's lore. It is at first a challenge to acclimate to the slow seduction and melancholy tone of Williams' world, but it is a challenge well worth undertaking.<br /><br />While I'd never say that <i>To Green Angel Tower</i> carries on at a blistering pace (Sanderson's action-forward, hard-to-put-down fantasy this is not), the book deals heavily in revelation, long-awaited confrontation, and some exciting reunions. The book, more or less, opens with a group of our heroes a few days before a messy battle for the Stone of Farewell. From there, the book alternates between great action scenes, interesting lore bits, and answers to questions I've had since the first book. There's still some travelogues that go on a bit too long, but its much more streamlined here than it ever was in the first two books.<br /><br />It's also really tough to tie things together in a meaningful fashion. Luckily, <i>To Green Angel Tower</i> contains some great surprises while delivering a conclusion that left me both pleased and hungry for more. Luckily, Williams has once more taken up the pen in service of Osten Ard and his sequel trilogy is already in motion. Though I'll probably take a bit of a break, I think I'll be checking in on these characters sooner than I would have originally anticipated.<br /><br />All in all, a great conclusion to a very worthwhile trilogy. I'm glad I stuck it out with this one, it'll remain a favourite fantasy world of mine for some time to come. <br /><br />[4.5 Stars]<br /><br />*Still planning on a trilogy-spanning review to be published sometime soon.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7549404773350133711" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Terence" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Terence" href="/user/show/1019174-terence">Terence</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer23814485" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7889521774865877816"><b>[Second read: March-April 2017]:</b> DNF<br/><br/><i>Memory, Sorrow & Thorn</i> has not aged well for me. I had to give it up less than 200 pages into <i>To Green Angel Tower</i>. If I were the type to go back and adjust ratings, I’d give four stars to Williams’ later <i>Shadowmarch</i> trilogy, and three for this. <i>Shadowmarch</i> is essentially the same story as <i>MS&T</i> but it’s so much more focused on essential plot without wandering down trackless paths of unnecessary exposition and subplot.<br/><br/>While reading, I was thinking to myself tha</span>
  <span id="freeText7889521774865877816" style="display:none"><b>[Second read: March-April 2017]:</b> DNF<br /><br /><i>Memory, Sorrow &amp; Thorn</i> has not aged well for me. I had to give it up less than 200 pages into <i>To Green Angel Tower</i>. If I were the type to go back and adjust ratings, I’d give four stars to Williams’ later <i>Shadowmarch</i> trilogy, and three for this. <i>Shadowmarch</i> is essentially the same story as <i>MS&amp;T</i> but it’s so much more focused on essential plot without wandering down trackless paths of unnecessary exposition and subplot.<br /><br />While reading, I was thinking to myself that this is what <i>The Lord of the Rings</i> would have been like if Tolkien had included the battles of Lothlorien, Rivendell, Mirkwood <i>and</i> the Lonely Mountain with all the minor characters and subplots that that would entail, instead of properly consigning them to brief mentions in the Appendices.<br /><br />I’m still looking forward to reading <i>The Heart of What Was Lost</i> when I find the time. The fact that <i>Shadowmarch</i> is so much better than <i>MS&amp;T</i> shows me that Williams has grown as a writer and that the good stuff I remembered from my first read wasn’t an illusion.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7889521774865877816" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Taylor" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4519828-taylor"><img alt="Taylor" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1485667166p2/4519828.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2427987926?book_show_action=true">Dec 30, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Taylor" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Taylor" href="/user/show/4519828-taylor">Taylor</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 0756402983" href="/book/show/33898847-to-green-angel-tower">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2427987926" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14801757575065040748">To Green Angel Tower is an action packed, exciting and all-around excellent ending to Tad Williams' Memory, Sorrow, and Thorn trilogy. However, there were definitely times throughout the book that I felt like To Green Angel Tower was longer than it needed to be and occasionally featured scenes that felt redundant, with multiple scenes kind of feeling like a repeat of previous scenes throughout the book, and I felt like that didn't exactly help move the plot forward when that happened. That being</span>
  <span id="freeText14801757575065040748" style="display:none">To Green Angel Tower is an action packed, exciting and all-around excellent ending to Tad Williams' Memory, Sorrow, and Thorn trilogy. However, there were definitely times throughout the book that I felt like To Green Angel Tower was longer than it needed to be and occasionally featured scenes that felt redundant, with multiple scenes kind of feeling like a repeat of previous scenes throughout the book, and I felt like that didn't exactly help move the plot forward when that happened. That being said, all of the storylines were enjoyable; especially when it came to the development of Simon and Miriamele's relationship, and the Josua/Vorzheva storyline. Even though it has its weaknesses, To Green Angel Tower is ultimately still a really great book. :)<br /><br />My rating/score: 4 out 5 stars on Goodreads. 8 out of 10 on my own scoring system.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14801757575065040748" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Xara Niouraki" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Xara Niouraki" href="/user/show/14432147-xara-niouraki">Xara Niouraki</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer904493072" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer765666826437564467">As a whole, it was an entertaining series. I enjoyed the feeling of classic fantasy that it gave me, especially since I've been reading a lot of dark fantasy lately. I was satisfied with the conclusion, but I had several problems with the third book.<br/><br/>First of all, it is huge. I don't have a problem with big books, but this one made me wish that it were smaller. I grew tired of it as I was reading it.<br/><br/>Secondly, the series is full of repetitions. I felt that half the pages of the series were descrip</span>
  <span id="freeText765666826437564467" style="display:none">As a whole, it was an entertaining series. I enjoyed the feeling of classic fantasy that it gave me, especially since I've been reading a lot of dark fantasy lately. I was satisfied with the conclusion, but I had several problems with the third book.<br /><br />First of all, it is huge. I don't have a problem with big books, but this one made me wish that it were smaller. I grew tired of it as I was reading it.<br /><br />Secondly, the series is full of repetitions. I felt that half the pages of the series were descriptions... How many times do I have to read a description of the underground corridors of Asua??? How many times did Simon come close to insanity??? The thing I hated most was the repetition of vocabulary. I literally hate the word mooncalf after reading the series....<br /><br />Most of the women in the book (not that there were many)were annoying. The worst one was Miriamele. I wish I could punch her in the face. She was constantly running away without consulting anyone. <br /><br />Morgenes manuscript: Ok Morgenes was wise and wrote the manuscript to help everyone. The thing that bothered me was that the characters kept reading the same thing again and again and discovering new clues and info. Maybe if they had more sources of info I would be more convinced.<br /><br />Finally, the characters were plain stupid many times. Many things were in front of their eyes and they kept talking and arguing and did something totally dumb in the end. <br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="765666826437564467" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer3587414763413320260">It feels so good to finally be able to finish a fantasy series! So many of the ones I'm currently reading aren't finished and its so frustrating having to wait years for the story to continue. To green angel tower, part 2 is the second half of the final book in the Memory, Sorrow and Thorn trilogy. It was exactly what I've been waiting for while reading series. The first two books of the trilogy were kind of a roller-coaster, good at times but I also find myself daydreaming during other parts of</span>
  <span id="freeText3587414763413320260" style="display:none">It feels so good to finally be able to finish a fantasy series! So many of the ones I'm currently reading aren't finished and its so frustrating having to wait years for the story to continue. To green angel tower, part 2 is the second half of the final book in the Memory, Sorrow and Thorn trilogy. It was exactly what I've been waiting for while reading series. The first two books of the trilogy were kind of a roller-coaster, good at times but I also find myself daydreaming during other parts of the books. The final book was very exciting throughout almost the whole book and even though the ending was somewhat anti-climactic I still enjoyed it and I finished very satisfied.<br /><br /><br />My favorite part of this series?<br />What these books lack in action they make up for in character development. You really get to know each and every one of the main characters and they are pretty believable as well. When I finished the series it was bittersweet because I knew it was the last time I'd read about these characters.<br /><br />I give this book 4.5/5 stars. At times, Tad Williams showed signs of brilliance in it but I think the book falls short of being 5 stars. For the series I give 4/5 stars. Even though it wasn't amazing it was still pretty good. I'm glad I decided to stick with it after the awful first 400 pages of the Dragonbone Chair.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3587414763413320260" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_387368882" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/387368882" />
    <a title="Earl Grey Tea" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4841374-earl-grey-tea"><img alt="Earl Grey Tea" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1296563240p2/4841374.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/387368882?book_show_action=true">Aug 08, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Earl Grey Tea" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Earl Grey Tea" href="/user/show/4841374-earl-grey-tea">Earl Grey Tea</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4841374-earl-grey-tea?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer387368882" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4985287888465076535">I don't put down a book or a series that I start. While the first two books did peak my interest quite a bit, I was suffering through almost all of the 1,104 pages found in this behemoth of a tale. Maybe I should have read it in two parts as it was released in its corporeal form and taken a break half way through. Instead, I went for the e-book version that doesn't suffer any publication problems when it comes to size.<br/><br/>By the time I was about a third of the way through the book, I was already bur</span>
  <span id="freeText4985287888465076535" style="display:none">I don't put down a book or a series that I start. While the first two books did peak my interest quite a bit, I was suffering through almost all of the 1,104 pages found in this behemoth of a tale. Maybe I should have read it in two parts as it was released in its corporeal form and taken a break half way through. Instead, I went for the e-book version that doesn't suffer any publication problems when it comes to size.<br /><br />By the time I was about a third of the way through the book, I was already burnt out. Tad Williams spent a lot of time and effort in the first two books to create his world, but for this last book it seems as if he was trying to finish the story so that it would fit into one book. I think that this book could have easily been written in three large books with plenty of detail and development of the story.<br /><br />Also, some of Williams' writing in this book seemed sophomoric. I lost count of the number of bad similes that I found, such as, "His eyes were sad like a lizard's." Through out this book, there were quite a few group discussions between the characters about what to do next. After a brief explanation by what would naturally be considered an unreliable source, the leader of the group is quick to declare that they trust this person and will follow their advice. I find this type of logic unrealistic, and it pulls me out of the story. Another time, one of the characters refused to enter a cavern. Instead of sounding like a person deeply troubled by some past experience (which I believe was the author's intent), the person ended up sounding like a pouting teenager. The overall human interaction seemed a bit whitewashed and oversimplified. Many of the characters' actions seemed too idealized.<br /><br />While there were some interesting ideas presented in this book, I'm glad that it is over. It was an experience for me; I saw some more of the world of fantasy literature, but I'm quite doubtful that I will pick up another Tad Williams book in the future.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4985287888465076535" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

I met him once, and he is also hilariously funny and a good guy.<br/><br/>So, I can not give an unbiased review, hee hee. :-) Only praise for this series and everything else Tad writes.<br/><br/>I was looking for fantasy books like Lord of the Rings when someone recommended me this. And while you shouldn't compare anything to Tolkien in terms of writing (my humble opinion), these books are a whole different category. So I can </span>
  <span id="freeText14129690509466257661" style="display:none">This is one of my favourite series, ever. Tad Williams is my favourite author of fantasy. I met him once, and he is also hilariously funny and a good guy.<br /><br />So, I can not give an unbiased review, hee hee. :-) Only praise for this series and everything else Tad writes.<br /><br />I was looking for fantasy books like Lord of the Rings when someone recommended me this. And while you shouldn't compare anything to Tolkien in terms of writing (my humble opinion), these books are a whole different category. So I can not say this is better than Lord of the Rings, or LotR is better than this, but it's alongside each other, just as great, but just different. I found these books to be easier to read, while LotR is more intense. <br /><br />I will stop comparing now, haha. Anyway, don't be daunted by the gazillion pages, if you're into fantasy you will breeze through them and after the three books you'll actually want more. So glad the author is still writing!</span>

<div id="review_1807631308" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1807631308" />
    <a title="Dawn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/44434125-dawn"><img alt="Dawn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1591448167p2/44434125.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1807631308?book_show_action=true">Feb 27, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dawn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dawn" href="/user/show/44434125-dawn">Dawn</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/26047391-to-green-angel-tower">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44434125-dawn?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44434125-dawn?shelf=series-to-read-in-order">series-to-read-in-order</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44434125-dawn?shelf=clean-read">clean-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44434125-dawn?shelf=will-read-again">will-read-again</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44434125-dawn?shelf=heros-i-love">heros-i-love</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44434125-dawn?shelf=all-time-favorite">all-time-favorite</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44434125-dawn?shelf=beta-hero">beta-hero</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44434125-dawn?shelf=virgin-heros">virgin-heros</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1807631308" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16278134967109554556">++SPOILERS++<br/><br/>This is the third book in the series, and this edition contains both parts 1 & 2 of the Green Angel Tower. There is a 4th book out and I plan to jump into that one soon.<br/><br/>Even though there were momments I felt frustrated over certains aspects of the story, overall this series was excellent.<br/><br/>The world building was incredible! The pace was good and although it was over a thousand pages, I couldn't put the book down. Yes there were times I skimmed and it did seem at times the overdescripti</span>
  <span id="freeText16278134967109554556" style="display:none">++SPOILERS++<br /><br />This is the third book in the series, and this edition contains both parts 1 &amp; 2 of the Green Angel Tower. There is a 4th book out and I plan to jump into that one soon.<br /><br />Even though there were momments I felt frustrated over certains aspects of the story, overall this series was excellent.<br /><br />The world building was incredible! The pace was good and although it was over a thousand pages, I couldn't put the book down. Yes there were times I skimmed and it did seem at times the overdescriptiveness got to me from time to time, but it was only here and there. Mostly I enjoyed the details which gave it depth and a reality.<br /><br />I think what I love most about long fantasy novels is the closeness I feel with the characters. When the story ends, I feel sad and will miss them. This is how I feel with this series.<br /><br />The friendship between Binabik and Simon is sweet and touching. I really felt their brotherly love of each other.<br /><br />The only negative I have is with the romantic aspect. Being a lover of romance novels, I am sure to be overly critical, but even other reviewers, I think, seemed to have the same feelings.<br /><br />Miriamele. I didn't understand, or think reasonable, her lousy treatment of Simon for most of the series. Why did he love her? Her character was great in book one, book two her and Simon were separated for all of it, and this one, she was reunited with Simon around 35% and was just mean to him..cold..until the near end.<br /><br />She pushes him away in a most hurtful way, intentionally, because she didn't feel worthy of him after she gave herself to Aspitis. It is understandable, her reasons, but more should have been done to show her feelings, at least to the reader so we don't hate her. I mean this author writes pages and pages of details about the enviornment, and actions yet when it comes to emotions, he just throws out crumbs.<br /><br />Her actions were cruel and unrealistic IMHO. His reaction to finding out about her lost innocence was realistic. He was pissed, and rightfully so. Especially after he admitted to being in love with her. So now they are distant emotionally (AGAIN) with each other, until she finally realizes what an ass she was and that she loves him. They don't reconcile until 97% and she never once apologized for treating him so shitty nor explained why she did. Ok she did save his life in the end, but Geeze, she blows him off and doesn't go to see him when he was recovering from his wounds? <br /><br />But, they did end up together, married, King and Queen. Totally in love. Lets just say the execution of the romance could be vastly improved. <br /><br />I am glad that Josua does finally love his woman whose name I can't spell. And I am also glad Simon remained innocent for Miriamele even though she didn't deserve it. <br /><br />Now I got that off my chest.<br /><br />Overall, romance aside, the story was fantastic, and I still loved it.<br /><br />Safety: Blood, gore,violence, torture. Sex off camera not detailed with Simon and Miriamele. No cursing.<br /><br />Romance peeps: Aside from what I mentioned above. Simon Kisses another girl while drunk and the slut Sithi Aditu kisses him "like a lover" but he has no feeling for her. But of course Miriamele had already screwed Aspitis so you can't blame him and it was before he even knew if Miriamele was even alive. No other women interaction outside of that occurs except with Miriamele.<br />Once Miriamele comes back and Simon sees her, neither are with any other person except each other.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16278134967109554556" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText16371054213156525254" style="display:none">OK, just as a matter of timing, I managed to finish all three Memory, Sorrow, and Thorn books while I was engaged in various travels with little or no Internet access and with no access to a decent keyboard, so I won't be composing full reviews. Instead I'll just say: George R.R. Martin has said that the Williams books were what spurred him to write A Song of Ice and Fire, and I do think that if you draw a line connecting Tolkien to Martin, Williams falls almost exactly midway between. The books are clearly epic fantasy (and I do mean EPIC, both in what happens and in word count), but they're not just slavishly aping Lord of the Rings; they're written as more of a response to or conversation with Lord of the Rings.<br /><br />And short of Tolkien himself (or possibly Terry Pratchett), Williams writes the best elves I remember finding -- they're genuinely alien, often aloof and sometimes disturbing.</span>

<div id="review_1805315125" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1805315125" />
    <a title="terpkristin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3392444-terpkristin"><img alt="terpkristin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1438465987p2/3392444.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1805315125?book_show_action=true">Nov 06, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="terpkristin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="terpkristin" href="/user/show/3392444-terpkristin">terpkristin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3392444-terpkristin?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3392444-terpkristin?shelf=sffaudio">sffaudio</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3392444-terpkristin?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3392444-terpkristin?shelf=kindle">kindle</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3392444-terpkristin?shelf=2017">2017</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1805315125" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8064004674530856641">Audiobook from Penguin Audio<br/>Narrated by Andrew Wincott<br/>Length: 63.25 hours<br/><br/>Tad Williams, it seems, is very good at writing <i>very long</i> books to end trilogies. This was a long book. <a href='https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/To_Green_Angel_Tower"' rel='nofollow noopener'>Wikipedia</a> says that it's one of the longest books ever written (though looking at the list of "longest books", there are many more that were even longer). I don't say this disparagingly, I say it so that readers of this review will be able to grok the scope covered by this book, which is almost as long as <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/91981.The_Dragonbone_Chair__Memory__Sorrow__and_Thorn___1_' title='The Dragonbone Chair (Memory, Sorrow, and Thorn, #1) by Tad Williams' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Dragonbone C</a></span>
  <span id="freeText8064004674530856641" style="display:none">Audiobook from Penguin Audio<br />Narrated by Andrew Wincott<br />Length: 63.25 hours<br /><br />Tad Williams, it seems, is very good at writing <i>very long</i> books to end trilogies. This was a long book. <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/To_Green_Angel_Tower&quot;" rel="nofollow noopener">Wikipedia</a> says that it's one of the longest books ever written (though looking at the list of "longest books", there are many more that were even longer). I don't say this disparagingly, I say it so that readers of this review will be able to grok the scope covered by this book, which is almost as long as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/91981.The_Dragonbone_Chair__Memory__Sorrow__and_Thorn___1_" title="The Dragonbone Chair (Memory, Sorrow, and Thorn, #1) by Tad Williams" rel="nofollow noopener">The Dragonbone Chair</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/28689.Stone_of_Farewell__Memory__Sorrow__and_Thorn___2_" title="Stone of Farewell (Memory, Sorrow, and Thorn, #2) by Tad Williams" rel="nofollow noopener">Stone of Farewell</a> put together. This lead to many hours of aural enjoyment, listening to Andrew Wincott narrate the story, but also lead to a lot to process. (Note: in my original review I noted that it was no surprise that this book was broken into two; it turns out that the hardcover release was always one book, but the paperback edition was broken into two).<br /><br />There isn't much to say about the third book in the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/series/49188-memory-sorrow-and-thorn" rel="nofollow noopener">Memory, Sorrow, and Thorn</a> trilogy that I didn't say in my review of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/194099101?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">book 1</a> or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1754999001" rel="nofollow noopener">book 2</a>. This is an epic fantasy trilogy that follows in the vein of Tolkien but takes cues from Nordic myth as well as, I think, European folklore. Years before <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/346732.George_R_R__Martin" title="George R.R. Martin" rel="nofollow noopener">George R.R. Martin</a> published <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13496.A_Game_of_Thrones__A_Song_of_Ice_and_Fire___1_" title="A Game of Thrones (A Song of Ice and Fire, #1) by George R.R. Martin" rel="nofollow noopener">A Game of Thrones</a>, a couple of years before <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/6252.Robert_Jordan" title="Robert Jordan" rel="nofollow noopener">Robert Jordan</a> published <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/228665.The_Eye_of_the_World__Wheel_of_Time___1_" title="The Eye of the World (Wheel of Time, #1) by Robert Jordan" rel="nofollow noopener">The Eye of the World</a>, and contemporary to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/60177.Guy_Gavriel_Kay" title="Guy Gavriel Kay" rel="nofollow noopener">Guy Gavriel Kay</a>, Williams started a series of books that would have many of the hallmarks of the books by these other authors...all in one series. Given that the first book was published in 1988, it seems likely that many people my age (and probably some who are older) go their first taste of "modern" epic fantasy with this series.<br /><br />Because I don't want to spoil anything in this review, I'll simply note that this is a deep series. The characters are multi-dimensional; they feel like real people instead of simply caricatures. Their motivations sometimes start simply, even innocently, but take on bigger life as the story unfolds. Some of the images in the trilogy are obvious, some are less so. Over the course of the three books, the reader loves, loses, and probably even cries...and certainly there are some good times, too. Veteran fantasy readers will recognize elements in common with a lot of other books out there, but will still enjoy it. For me, it was a comforting read that offered new experiences along with the familiar ones. <br /><br />Once I got the character names down (see my comments in the review of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/194099101?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">The Dragonbone Chair</a>), this clearly became a "must listen" series, even though it could be consumed more quickly (for me, anyway) in print. I really enjoyed Wincott's narration style and accent; they felt like they "belonged" with this story. If you have the opportunity, I strongly recommend listening to it. I look forward to what's next in Osten Ard and I hope that Wincott narrates it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8064004674530856641" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2589165740" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2589165740" />
    <a title="Michael Battaglia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17248131-michael-battaglia"><img alt="Michael Battaglia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1360285538p2/17248131.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2589165740?book_show_action=true">Nov 23, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael Battaglia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael Battaglia" href="/user/show/17248131-michael-battaglia">Michael Battaglia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2589165740" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3784792682795127774">Okay, Tad Williams, I'll admit it . . . you wore me down.<br/><br/>Each of these books so far in the trilogy has individually been longer than other entire trilogies and with each book Williams seemed to be in a contest to one-up his page count each time, probably necessitating a generous Christmas/birthday/Arbor Day gift to his editor for some years to come after the publication of this. A spine cracking thousand pages plus, in paperback it was originally too big for mortal bindings and was published in </span>
  <span id="freeText3784792682795127774" style="display:none">Okay, Tad Williams, I'll admit it . . . you wore me down.<br /><br />Each of these books so far in the trilogy has individually been longer than other entire trilogies and with each book Williams seemed to be in a contest to one-up his page count each time, probably necessitating a generous Christmas/birthday/Arbor Day gift to his editor for some years to come after the publication of this. A spine cracking thousand pages plus, in paperback it was originally too big for mortal bindings and was published in two parts (paving the way for all those movie trilogies that magically happen to expand to two parts for the finale) but eventually technology caught up to its girth and you can acquire it in one blunt force trauma inducing edition. And frankly, it works better that way. Even though there's enough book in here for four other books, plowing through this one page by page almost locks you into a battle of wills with its length as you and the book dare each other to blink first. Splitting it into two more manageable five hundred page helpings almost feels like cheating, like bumpers in a bowling alley. If you need the crutch then maybe this isn't the series for you.<br /><br />And we have come a long way. Just the covers will tell you, at least on my editions (I have the original Michael Whelan covers, as opposed to the more minimalist sword covers that seem to be current) . . . when we started way back in "The Dragonbone Chair" the cover featured a babyfaced Simon frantically carrying a small man sporting an arrow in his gut that we later found out was Binabik the troll, while bringing up the rear is the disguised Princess Miriamele, equally adolescent and also featuring a "oh crap" expression on her face.<br /><br />Compare that to our last cover, where our two heroes stare at you as grizzled veterans, holding their weapons with utmost confidence, Simon wearing torn clothing that suggests "you should see the other guy" while Miriamele gives us her best "come at me, bro" look. My, they grow up fast.<br /><br />Fortunately for the reader, its not too, too fast. Let's note that the book is called "*To* Green Angel Tower", implying the thrill is going to be in the journey. And boy howdy is there a lot of journey. The first half of the book mostly consists of getting the band back together, so we flip between Prince Josua, Simon, Binabik and company hanging out at the Stone of Farewell and Miriamele's twin frustrations of being on the worst first date ever with a nobleman that has no word for "let's slow down" in his language and Inebriated Emo Priest Cadrach. Stuck on a boat with those two and surrounded by what are basically Murder Seals, its almost a given that she'd trade with Simon and the lads in a heartbeat.<br /><br />And actually for the first chunk of the book her story winds up being more interesting than anything Simon is going through. Faced with an army whose rules of engagement start at "total annihilation", Williams' hand with characterizations helps keep things moving but if you've read one epic fantasy battle you aren't going to find anything new here. Meanwhile, Miriamele's travails are far more complex, as she tries to navigate a situation with no easy way out while being saddled with extraordinarily low self-esteem and a less than helpful traveling companion. Then when that does resolve that problem she picks up a far more varied cast and acquits herself well in the several ludicrously lethal situations they find themselves in, often winding up being steady and clever and the closest the team has to an adult at times considering that everyone with her is twice her age. The sheer variety of locales and dangers in her plot at least keeps things lively.<br /><br />Lest you think this is all moving quickly, rest assured its not. The swordplay and close-calls are fun but what kept me here through the millennial length was his skill at characterization, the sheer sincerity of the characters and how real their relationships feel even as the stakes grow higher. As they plan and argue and reminisce and puzzle through their various dilemmas you can feel the weight of their shared histories, both what you've experienced with them and what existed before the books started. One aspect that I found fascinating was how well he wrote relationships, not something fantasy at the time was known for doing brilliantly well. Whether it’s the decades of love between Isgrimnur and his wife Gutrun or the more fiery pairing of Josua and Vorzheva, the newly strong love of Binabik and Sisqi or the "will they or-oh who are we kidding its just a matter of time" nervousness of Miriamele and Simon. He captures them all extremely well, putting each half of the couple on equal footing so you can easily understand what each might see in the other and how they manage to work despite differences. Since they're the heroes, Simon and Miriamele's shy creep toward each other often takes center stage and he plays it well to make it seem natural despite the lopsidedness of available options (while several women have their eye on Simon, Miriamele's suitors range from people who want to seduce her for political reasons or those who want to seduce her for lecherous reasons, none of whom are anywhere near her age . . . its bad enough that when she breaks down at one point, cracking under the weight of her own loneliness, you honestly feel for the poor girl).<br /><br />He also has a good eye for shading the other races of Osten Ard as just different enough that you get a feel for their cultures without feeling like he's reading out of a Monster's Manual. The Sithi start out strange and remain consistently strange throughout the novel, the kind of people you can watch a comedy with and laugh at different parts and not quite understand why the other person is laughing where they are. Add in the resolutely weird dwarrows, the off-kilter Niskie and the killing machines that are the Norns and the trolls seem downright normal.<br /><br />Is it too much for one book? Probably. Its almost too much for one series and some might find the second half of the book bogs down a bit as the cast splits and splits again while Williams stretches out some plots to play for time in order to get everyone back into position. His tendency to make you feel like you're experiencing the book at the same pace as the characters . . . a lengthy torture sequence appears to occur in real time, as well as another endless escape through tunnels that winds up being alternating scenes of fascinating and terrifying architecture. He also doesn't help his own cause by creating roadblocks that shouldn't be as roadblocky . . . Miriamele's cockeyed plan in particular not only has "doomed" written all over it but stands a very chance of getting everyone she knows killed in the process, yet instead of doing the sensible thing and tying her up to keep her from enacting it, anyone who finds out goes along with it anyway. I understand not letting her go on a suicide mission alone but there's a fine line between "support" and "enabling". Two separate characters have mysterious pasts that they don't want to talk about yet remind us constantly how they're too traumatized to talk about it . . . one guy in particular probably could have shortened the novel by about two hundred pages if he had just spoken up at almost any point in the story.<br /><br />In fact it seems clear at times that Williams was worrying about the story first and the themes later, which means the tale may not resonate on as mythic a level as Tolkien's does. Williams wants to play with the idea that the stories that golden ages are built upon may not be true but those revelations, when they do come, either arrive too late to make a difference or have a negligible impact on the plot. He also appears to be think of more expansive roles for characters on the fly, meaning that after spending passage upon passage on certain minor characters over three novels, we find they have only a minor effect on the climax (and in one case the character is entirely absent). My personal favorite scene in this regard is a bit toward the end when some of the main cast try to put their heads together to resolve contradictions in the plans of their enemies, as if Williams was saying it was part of the scheme all along. Hey, I've been there, no need to apologize to me.<br /><br />But ultimately, do you know what? Absolutely none of these matters because when the time comes to pull this all together he one hundred percent nails it, not sticking the landing so much as doing a triple axel double somersault pirouette with enough grace that even Mary Lou Retton would acknowledge with a respectful nod. Asked to somehow sum up the various plots and conflicts and interpersonal differences in a twenty four hundred page epic, he crafts a climax and a resolution that somehow hits every high point possible, working your every emotion in a way that's frighteningly self-assured in how natural it feels. Too many epics I've read don't know how to properly wrap things up and leave us feeling like the all the effort was worth it and yet not only does Williams effortlessly get us where we need to be but somehow manages to make you feel the combined weight of every page that's come before.<br /><br />Its astounding how much this one sticks with me. Its been two days since I finished it before going to work and when I got home I read those last twenty or so pages again to see if the impact wasn't a fluke. If anything, it was stronger. It still quivers in me now, in a way an ending hasn't for a long time (Guy Kay's "Lions of Al-Rassan" comes close but that was far more melancholy). And while the story has something to do with it, and the setting has something to do with it, ultimately for me it came down to the characters. Simply put, by the end of this epic he makes you give a crap about these people . . . you spend so much time with them watching them struggle for every last inch that you're begging for them to succeed, you're praying for them to get through this and use the chance to make a better world than the one they came into. You feel a warmth for these people and the warmth they have for each other, a bond that only those who have been through what they've been through can ever understand. The ending reminds you how much has changed since the start, how many of these people started out this series as total strangers to each other and how much they've gotten to know each other in the interim. Its more than just the fireworks of romantic love finding its own voice finally, its two scholars reveling in their own quiet joys, its mourners celebrating the departed and their own miraculously fragile existences, its lives interrupted finally getting to start again, its second chances at reinvention finally getting taken, it’s a joy that acknowledges the almost unbearable pain it took to get there and the understanding that it can't be squandered. Its everything you want for these people because when its done you care a hell of a lot about them.<br /><br />Will the ending be worth it for everyone? I don't know. It’s a lot a book to get there and I can't say its all essential. Maybe I'm just getting sentimental in my old age. We all have our weak spots and maybe this is mine. But I came close to tearing up at the end of this, feeling strongly enough about these people that while I didn't want to see them put through another physical and emotional wringer like this series, I'd be more than happy to read stories of their day to day lives just interacting with each other, which is probably the literary equivalent of being willing to hear someone sing the telephone directory. I could let them go and be done, leave them as they are and be content with that.<br /><br />And yet in one of life's weird coincidences, just as I get to a series I bought over a decade ago, I see he's starting another trilogy with these characters and the next generations, twenty-five years after this finished, asking you to go back and risk undoing perfection. And all I can think is: well, all right. If you insist.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3784792682795127774" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1202674547" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1202674547" />
    <a title="David H." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18844431-david-h"><img alt="David H." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1551048918p2/18844431.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1202674547?book_show_action=true">Jun 14, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David H." class="user" itemprop="url" name="David H." href="/user/show/18844431-david-h">David H.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18844431-david-h?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1202674547" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4218357204273957681">What can one say about the final book of an epic fantasy trilogy without spoiling too much of what came before? Tad Williams really knows how to luxuriate in his setting (and that's not always a good thing), and this particular novel on its own was one of the longest single fantasy books written at approximately 550,000 words (not even Jordan, Martin, Sanderson, or Erikson ever matched that).<br/><br/>It's definitely better than the first two in general pacing, though (my usual complaint about the earlier</span>
  <span id="freeText4218357204273957681" style="display:none">What can one say about the final book of an epic fantasy trilogy without spoiling too much of what came before? Tad Williams really knows how to luxuriate in his setting (and that's not always a good thing), and this particular novel on its own was one of the longest single fantasy books written at approximately 550,000 words (not even Jordan, Martin, Sanderson, or Erikson ever matched that).<br /><br />It's definitely better than the first two in general pacing, though (my usual complaint about the earlier volumes), as Williams heads to the end game. Honestly, I wish the series had been structured that the plots of the first two were shortened and this one expanded. <br /><br />I really end up loving a lot of the main characters (and one of my favorite minor characters lived! Thanks, Tad!). The author really put them through the wringer. I do wish that there had been more and more prominent women--I feel like there were only three or so that got significant POV time, and others were simply wives or other nobodies (contrast with Robert Jordan's Wheel of Time series, which, for all its faults, has a million woman with significant page time).<br /><br />If you've made it through the first two books, you might as well read To Green Angel Tower as it's a better read than Stone of Farewell (as things actually get accomplished). If you're not sure if you want to even start this trilogy, I'm not sure how I'd guide you--two of my friends recently quit the first book barely ten minutes in, and for others it's their foundational epic fantasy. I can see both sides.<br /><br />As an aside, I read these books for an online read-along where we'd read about 100 pages a week. Technically speaking I'm not supposed to finish until August, but I found that I find myself frustrated at going at that pace because it interrupts my flow when I take a break from reading other things to read for the read-along. However, I do appreciate that it made me consider the book in a deeper light as I paused to consider the book/story several times per volume (15 times for this book!) instead of my more usual "binge" style.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4218357204273957681" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1281340587" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1281340587" />
    <a title="Edward Rathke" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5657249-edward-rathke"><img alt="Edward Rathke" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442510992p2/5657249.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1281340587?book_show_action=true">May 15, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Edward Rathke" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Edward Rathke" href="/user/show/5657249-edward-rathke">Edward Rathke</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780886775216" href="/book/show/317451.To_Green_Angel_Tower">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1281340587" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8047633698525401436">This is one of the longest novels ever written. It's the third in a trilogy, but it's about as long as the first two novels combined, making it a very odd but very satisfying final chapter.<br/><br/>Each step of this series has been pretty difficult for me to meaningfully rate, but I'll use this rating of this book to do the whole series.<br/><br/>I think each book is about a four but the series as a whole is a five, if only because of how complex and beautiful and awesome it all is. <br/><br/>The worldbuilding is probably s</span>
  <span id="freeText8047633698525401436" style="display:none">This is one of the longest novels ever written. It's the third in a trilogy, but it's about as long as the first two novels combined, making it a very odd but very satisfying final chapter.<br /><br />Each step of this series has been pretty difficult for me to meaningfully rate, but I'll use this rating of this book to do the whole series.<br /><br />I think each book is about a four but the series as a whole is a five, if only because of how complex and beautiful and awesome it all is. <br /><br />The worldbuilding is probably some of the best I've seen in any series. The cultures are complex with their own customs, religions, and histories. It never feels cheap or shortcutty either. It's all there for you to dig into, even though much of each culture goes unexplained. He even goes so far as to invent really specific curses for each culture, which just feels right.<br /><br />One of my favorite things is how Williams deals with religion, because it's not an overt aspect. It's just this net that kind of stretches under the world for most humans. There's a dominant religion in Ostern Ard, the continent this takes place on. It's super similar to christianity in practice, though customs are heavily borrowed from Norse mythology as well. The religion is brought up often because it's a central aspect of most of the characters' lives. This is sort of a medieval setting so it's like christianity in medieval europe: most people believe it. There's no focus on it being untrue or true. It's never really put into focus. The trilogy doesn't dwell on religion, except to show how it shapes a person's worldview and how they may come to lean on it in times of misery and calamity. It's a subtle thing he does and I really appreciate how Williams chooses not to spit on why people believe the things they do, which is something all too common. But this religion isn't the only one, and like most europeans in medieval europe who had to share borders with people of other religions, they're gently tolerated. Sure, characters call them heathens and so on, but they're allowed their beliefs.<br /><br />The characters are well developed and well loved by the end. Though the first book does a lot of telling you who is good and evil, the series becomes much more amoral as it goes. Good characters must do evil and evil characters find themselves doing good. It shows how Williams grew as a writer and storyteller in the writing process. I mean, how could he not? The series is like 3,500 pages.<br /><br />I really dig this narrative style. There are times in this third novel where it felt like fifty page chunks could have been condensed without losing anything, but I was also okay with it. I think I only began to feel that way because I read half the book in airports and on airplanes and kind of just wanted to finish it so I could stop carrying the five pound monster around. But it's an easy going style that flows forward. This was probably the novel with the most forward momentum, or at least with the most happening.<br /><br />It's interesting, too, how Williams will almost skip action scenes. It would feel like a cheat, but I think it's more subtle than that. Something more akin to Malick's Thin Red Line. Because this story has never been about the ability to kill or fight. It's been about intelligence, history, and putting pieces together.<br /><br />I've said before that I could see how the series would end and that that didn't matter, because it's the journey that counts for these books. I still think that, but this final volume had plenty of surprises in there to keep me going. Even the ending had a fair share of surprises. I'd actually like to discuss that ending because it's one of the most interesting ones I've ever read, and it will definitely lead to a love or hate kind of effect.<br /><br />I mostly find it interesting, but wholly understand why a lot of people might hate it.<br /><br />The series is very satisfying in many ways. Though it began as a story about scholars and servants, it becomes much more traditional as it goes. It's a story of heroism with many of the characters being nobility of some kind, but the scholars actually remain some of the most important characters. Other important characters are thieves, drunkards, scoundrels, shaman, and so on, but this is very much an aristocratic story by the end, which is kind of disappointing, but some of the characters have very interesting things to say about the nature of nobility, which I greatly appreciated.<br /><br />Williams has a great understanding about what motivates people, and how it's often the story that matters more than the truth, and this is shown brightly throughout this volume.<br /><br />I really loved this series and there are a lot of things I want to talk about but I won't write it here because of spoilers and so on, but I recommend this highly. If you finish the first book and dig it, you should read to the end. It's a very interesting take on epic fantasy and so worth the stamina you'll need to plow through all these pages.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8047633698525401436" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1927207938" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1927207938" />
    <a title="Robert Cooke" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/61679660-robert-cooke"><img alt="Robert Cooke" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1488277414p2/61679660.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1927207938?book_show_action=true">Feb 28, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Robert Cooke" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Robert Cooke" href="/user/show/61679660-robert-cooke">Robert Cooke</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1927207938" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11298629453138885339">I recently devoured the 'Memory, Sorrow, Thorn' series having read and enjoyed Tad Williams 'Shadowmarch' series. I have to say I did enjoy this better, with Green Angel Tower providing a fitting climax to the series. Strangely when reading 'Shadowmarch' I found that the lore of the world richer than the characters, here I found it somewhat the reverse and could have happily had a bit more background history at certain points. Though that said part of the joy of the book for me was the central c</span>
  <span id="freeText11298629453138885339" style="display:none">I recently devoured the 'Memory, Sorrow, Thorn' series having read and enjoyed Tad Williams 'Shadowmarch' series. I have to say I did enjoy this better, with Green Angel Tower providing a fitting climax to the series. Strangely when reading 'Shadowmarch' I found that the lore of the world richer than the characters, here I found it somewhat the reverse and could have happily had a bit more background history at certain points. Though that said part of the joy of the book for me was the central characters labouring in ignorance at what they should be doing. The books do a great job of making magic something rarefied and well magical, knowledge is power as the saying goes and the series makes a point of highlighting this throughout. Williams as always builds an engaging world of diverse places and as a result I found the story very difficult to put down. With the book leaving a couple of loose threads open I would imagine I would be entirely annoyed right now had I read this book when it was initially released in 1993, as it is I feel I timed it right with Williams releasing a long awaited follow up series this year. If you've read Williams work before then you'll be in safe ground here and it's easy to see why this was his breakthrough series. If you're new to his work then this is an engaging and rewarding place to start. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11298629453138885339" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
